Section 11.2.10.1 - Unless otherwise permitted by Clause 11.2.10.3 or 11.2.10.4, the effective web width shall be taken as (Starts on Page 84)
the minimum concrete web width within depth d.

Section 11.2.10.2 - In determining the concrete web width at a particular level, one-half the diameters of ungrouted post- (Starts on Page 84)
tensioning ducts or one-quarter the diameters of grouted ducts at that level shall be subtracted from
the total web width.

Section 11.2.10.4 - For members with tapering webs, bw may be taken as the average web width calculated over a (Starts on Page 84)
contiguous height that includes the minimum web width location but does not include any regions of
the section where the side faces of the section slope outward at more than 20¬∞ from the direction of
the applied shear.

Section 11.2.10.5 - The minimum area of shear reinforcement specified by Equation 11.1 is calculated using the unspalled (Starts on Page 84)
web width. When the area of shear reinforcement exceeds eleven times this calculated minimum,
spalling of the concrete cover shall be accounted for in determining bw; otherwise spalling may be
neglected. If spalling is to be accounted for, the concrete cover down to the centerline of the outermost
reinforcement shall be assumed to have spalled off unless the ends of the compression diagonals are
restrained against spalling.

Section 11.2.11 - Reduced prestress in transfer length (Starts on Page 84)
In pretensioned members, the reduction in prestress in the transfer length of prestressing tendons shall
be considered when computing VP, fpo, and the tensile force that can be resisted by the longitudinal
reinforcement. The prestress force may be assumed to vary linearly from zero at the point at which
bonding commences to a maximum at a distance from the end of the tendon equal to the transfer
length, assumed to be 50 diameters for strand and 100 diameters for single wire.

the supporting beam.

Section 11.3.9.4 - Compression fan regions (Starts on Page 90)
In regions adjacent to maximum moment locations, the area of longitudinal reinforcement on the
flexural tension side of the member need not exceed the area required to resist the maximum moment
acting alone. This provision shall apply only if the support or the load at the maximum moment location
introduces direct compression into the flexural compression face of the member and the member is not
subject to significant torsion.

longitudinal axis and extending from the inside edge of the bearing area intersects the centroid of the
reinforcement. See Figure 11.5 b).

Section 11.4.1.1 - General (Starts on Page 91)
The strength of reinforced concrete structures, members, or regions may be investigated by idealizing
the reinforced concrete as a series of reinforcing steel tensile ties and concrete compressive struts
interconnected at nodes to form a truss capable of carrying all of the factored loads to the supports. In
determining the geometry of the truss, account shall be taken of the required dimensions of the struts
and ties.

Section 11.4.1.2 - Simplified and refined strut-and-tie models (Starts on Page 91)
The crack control reinforcement may also be used as tension ties in the strut-and-tie model provided
this reinforcement is well anchored in accordance with Clause 11.4.3.3 (see Figure 11.3).
reproduction interdite.

Section 11.4.1.3 - Modelling members subjected to uniform loads (Starts on Page 92)
In modelling members subjected to uniform loads, a series of struts shall be used to represent the areas
where the compressive stresses fan out from the bearing areas. [see Figure 11.4 a) and b)]. However,
where the uniform load is applied to the flexural compression face and the member contains crack
control reinforcement the fanning area can be modelled by a single strut located at the resultant of the
load [see Figure 11.4 c)].

b) Modelling of fanning compression with a series of struts ‚Äî Tension tie at wide part of fan.
c) Modelling each fanning area with a single strut for members containing crack control
reinforcement.

Section 11.4.3.3 - Anchorage of ties anchoring compressive struts (Starts on Page 96)
Tension ties anchoring compressive struts in regions away from bearing regions shall be detailed as

ties shall consist of closed stirrups with each bend enclosing a longitudinal bar.

Section 11.4.4.2 - Satisfying stress limits in node regions (Starts on Page 96)
The stress limits in node regions may be considered satisfied if
a)
the bearing stress on the node regions produced by concentrated loads or reactions does not
exceed the stress limits specified in Clause 11.4.4.1; and
b)
the tie reinforcement is uniformly distributed over an effective area of concrete at least equal to
the tie force divided by the stress limits specified in Clause 11.4.4.1.

Section 11.4.5 - Crack control reinforcement (Starts on Page 96)
Structures, members, or regions (other than slabs or footings) that have been designed in accordance
with Clause 11.4 shall contain an orthogonal grid of reinforcing bars near each face. The ratio of
reinforcement area to gross concrete area shall be not less than 0.002 in each direction. The spacing of
this reinforcement shall not exceed 300 mm. If located within the tie, the crack control reinforcement
may also be considered as tie reinforcement.

three ties or stirrup ties spaced along a length at least equal to the inside diameter of the hook at a
spacing not greater than 3db, where db is the nominal diameter of the hooked bar. For this case, the
factor specified in Clause 12.5.3 c) shall not apply.

Section 12.5.5 - Development of bars in compression (Starts on Page 102)
Hooks shall not be considered effective in developing bars in compression.

Section 12.6.1 - Any mechanical anchorage, including heads of headed bars or headed studs, demonstrated by test to be (Starts on Page 102)
capable of developing the strength of reinforcement without damage to the concrete, may be used.

Section 12.6.2 - The development of reinforcement may consist of a combination of mechanical anchorage and (Starts on Page 102)
additional embedment length of reinforcement between the point of maximum bar stress and the
mechanical anchorage.

Section 12.14.1 - Limitations on use (Starts on Page 106)
Splices of reinforcement shall be made only as required or permitted by design drawings or
specifications, or as authorized by the designer.

Section 12.14.2.1 - Lap splices shall not be used for bars larger than 35M, except as specified in Clauses 12.16.2 and (Starts on Page 106)
15.9.2.4.

Section 12.14.2.2 - Lap splices of bundled bars shall be based on the lap splice length required for individual bars within a (Starts on Page 106)
bundle, increased by 10% for a two-bar bundle, 20% for a three-bar bundle, and 33% for a four-bar
bundle. Individual bar splices within a bundle shall not overlap.

Section 12.14.2.3 - Bars spliced by lap splices in flexural members shall have a transverse spacing not exceeding the lesser (Starts on Page 106)
of one-fifth of the required lap splice length or 150 mm.

Section 12.14.3.3 - A full welded splice shall have bars welded to develop, in tension, at least 120% of the specified yield (Starts on Page 106)
strength, fy, of the bar, but not less than 110% of the actual yield strength of the bar used in the test of
the welded splice.

Section 12.14.3.4 - A full mechanical connection shall develop, in tension or compression as required, at least 120% of the (Starts on Page 106)
specified yield strength, fy, of the bar, but not less than 110% of the actual yield strength of the bar
used in the test of the mechanical connection.

Section 12.17.2 - Reinforcement (Starts on Page 108)
Where welded splices, mechanical connections, or end-bearing splices are used, the amount of

the gross area of reinforcement exceeds 0.04 times the gross area of the section, connection or splice
locations shall be spaced not less than 750 mm apart (see Clause 10.9.2).

and 12.16.2, and, where applicable, with Clause 12.17.3.4 or 12.17.3.5.

the area of the vertical reinforcement in that face.

Section 13.2.7 - Computation of slab deflections (Starts on Page 111)

üìê the concrete shall be taken as one-half of the value specified by Equation 8.3. Additional long-term
deflection shall be computed as specified in Clause 9.8.2.5.

Section 13.3.2 - One-way and two-way shear (Starts on Page 112)

shall also be investigated.

straight sides. For edge supports, the critical section may be assumed to have three straight sides. For
corner supports, the critical section may be assumed to have two straight sides. At edge and corner
supports where the slab cantilevers beyond the exterior face of the support, the critical section may be
assumed to extend into the cantilevered portion of the slab for a distance not exceeding d.

shall be considered ineffective.

Section 13.5 - Design procedures for flexure (Starts on Page 117)
Note: See also Clauses 13.6 to 13.12 and Annex B.

Section 13.5.1 - A slab system may be designed using any procedure satisfying conditions of equilibrium and (Starts on Page 117)
compatibility with the supports, provided that it is shown that the factored resistance at every section is
at least equal to the effects of the factored loads and that all serviceability conditions, including
specified limits on deflections, are met.

Section 13.5.2 - For lateral loads, analysis of unbraced frames shall take into account the effects of cracking and (Starts on Page 117)
reinforcement on stiffness of frame members.

Section 13.5.4 - Openings of any size may be provided in slab systems if it is shown by analysis that the factored (Starts on Page 117)
resistance is at least equal to the effects of factored loads in accordance with Clauses 8.3, 8.4, and
13.3.3.4, and that all serviceability conditions, including the specified limits on deflections, are met.
Note: Clause 13.10.10 provides simple rules for regular slabs without beams.

Section 13.6.1 - Analysis of slab systems may be based on elastic plate theory that uses either classical or numerical (Starts on Page 117)
techniques.
Note: The successful application of the results of analysis using elastic plate theory requires proper consideration of
factors such as selection of thickness, moment redistribution due to the effects of cracking, creep, shrinkage
effects, and construction loading.

Section 13.6.2 - Care shall be taken to ensure realistic modelling of the size and effective stiffness of the supporting (Starts on Page 117)
elements, including beams, if any.

Section 13.6.3 - Appropriate loading patterns shall be considered to ensure determination of maximum values for all (Starts on Page 117)
stress resultants at each section.

moments shall be adjusted to account for the effects of torsion. In lieu of more detailed calculations,
the design moment intensities, mx,des or my,des, in the x and y directions at any point shall be computed

Section 13.6.5 - Uniformly spaced reinforcement shall be placed in bands such that (Starts on Page 118)
a)
the total reinforcement provided within a band shall be sufficient to resist the total factored
moment computed for that band; and
b)

factored moment intensity within the band.
Note: Additional information on the application of finite element analysis design techniques is provided by the
Cement Association of Canada's Concrete design handbook.

Section 13.7.1 - Analysis of slab systems for factored loads may be based on either the lower bound or upper bound (Starts on Page 118)
theorems of plasticity.
Note: The successful application of the results of plastic analysis requires proper assumptions that will ensure that
serviceability requirements, including creep and shrinkage effects, are satisfied.

Section 13.7.3 - When strength design is based on the upper bound theorem (e.g., yield line method), the factored (Starts on Page 118)
moments shall be obtained from calculations based on a need for a mechanism to form over the whole
or part of the slab at collapse. The mechanism that is the most critical shall be used for the design of
the slab.

Section 13.7.4 - Factored moments obtained using lower bound theory (e.g., strip method) shall satisfy the (Starts on Page 118)
requirements of equilibrium and the boundary conditions applicable to the slab.

Section 13.7.5 - Reinforcement may be uniformly spaced in bands, with band widths selected to ensure that (Starts on Page 118)
serviceability requirements are satisfied.

Section 13.8.1.1 - A regular two-way slab system (see Clause 3.1) may, for purposes of analysis, be considered a series of (Starts on Page 119)

the slab continues beyond that point.

neglected.

Section 13.9.5 - Selection of reinforcement (Starts on Page 124)
Reinforcement to resist the moments at the critical sections shall be selected in accordance with

Section 13.10.1 - General (Starts on Page 124)
Reinforcement in each direction for two-way slab systems shall be determined from moments at critical
sections but shall be not less than that required by Clause 7.8.1.
Note: Where strict crack control is a concern, slabs with drop panels, particularly in a corrosive environment, can
require additional reinforcement in the negative middle strip region to limit cracking. This additional reinforcement
is not included in the calculation of moment resistance. The reinforcement required to limit cracking is generally
more than that required by Clause 7.8.1.

and column, a fraction of unbalanced moment given by

shall be transferred by flexural reinforcement placed within a width bb.

Clause 7.8.1 shall be provided in that section of the slab outside of the band region defined by bb, or as
required by Clause 13.10.9.

Note: See Clause 3.1 under ‚ÄúWall‚Äù for wall type definitions.

Section 14.1.1 - Application (Starts on Page 131)
Clauses 14.1.2 to 14.4.6 shall apply to the design of walls, except where the additional requirements
specified in Clauses 16 and 21 to 23 apply.

Section 14.1.2 - Lateral support of walls (Starts on Page 131)
Walls shall be considered laterally supported if
a)
walls or other vertical bracing elements are arranged in two directions so as to provide lateral
stability to the structure as a whole; and
b)
connections between the wall and its lateral supports are designed to resist a horizontal force not
less than 2% of the total factored vertical load that the wall is designed to carry at the level of the
lateral support, but not less than 5 kN per metre length of the wall.

Section 14.1.3.1 - In lieu of a detailed analysis, each concentrated compressive vertical load acting on a wall shall be (Starts on Page 131)
assumed to be uniformly distributed over a horizontal length ‚Ñìb of wall. At any position below the level
of the concentrated load, the portion of ‚Ñìb on each side of the centre of the concentrated load shall be
one-half of the width of the bearing plus the width enclosed by a line sloping downward at two vertical
to one horizontal on each side, limited by intersection with the end of the wall. This stressed width shall
reproduction interdite.
not be assumed to exceed nine times the wall thickness on each side of the bearing area. For a wall
subjected to more than one concentrated load, the design shall take into account the overlapping of
uniformly distributed loads from each of the concentrated loads.

Section 14.1.3.2 - Cracking resulting from transverse tensile stresses caused by the spread of the concentrated loads (Starts on Page 132)
acting on the wall shall be taken into account in the design.
Note: Strut-and-tie models can be used to compute the amount of reinforcement required to resist the transverse
force under bearing loads and to control cracking.

Section 14.1.4 - Columns built integrally with walls (Starts on Page 132)
Columns built integrally with walls shall be designed as specified in Clause 10, with outside dimensions
that comply with Clause 10.11.2.

Section 14.1.5 - Transfer of vertical wall loads through floor (Starts on Page 132)
When the specified compressive strength of the concrete in the walls, f‚Ä≤
cw, exceeds that specified for the
floor, f‚Ä≤
cs, the strength of a wall-to-floor joint shall be determined using the lower of the concrete
strengths in the wall and the floor. The strength of this joint can be increased by adding dowels or by
increasing the strength of the concrete in the floor under and adjacent to the wall. Such concrete shall
extend at least 500 mm into the floor from each face of the wall.

Section 14.1.6 - Transfer of horizontal wall forces across construction joints (Starts on Page 132)
Transfer of horizontal wall forces across construction joints shall be as specified in Clause 11.5. The area
of the reinforcement crossing the shear plane shall be the larger of
a)
the reinforcement area provided for flexure and axial loads; and
b)
the reinforcement area required for shear friction.
In flanged walls, only the vertical reinforcement in those portions of the section assumed to resist
horizontal shear shall be included in this calculation.

Section 14.1.7.1 - Bearing walls and shear walls (Starts on Page 132)
The thickness of bearing walls and the webs and flanges of shear walls shall be not less than the smaller
of ‚Ñìw/25 or hu/25, but not less than 150 mm.

Section 14.1.7.2 - Non-bearing walls (Starts on Page 132)
The thickness of non-bearing walls shall be not less than 1/30 of the unsupported height or length,
whichever is shorter, or less than 100 mm.

Section 14.1.8.1 - Distributed and concentrated reinforcement (Starts on Page 132)
Walls shall have distributed vertical and horizontal reinforcement in layers in accordance with
Clauses 14.1.8.2 to 14.1.8.7. Walls shall also have concentrated vertical reinforcement in accordance
with Clause 14.1.8.8.
reproduction interdite.

Section 14.1.8.2 - Maximum diameter of distributed reinforcement (Starts on Page 133)
The diameter of bars used for distributed reinforcement shall not exceed one-tenth of the wall
thickness.

Section 14.1.8.3 - Number of layers of wall reinforcement (Starts on Page 133)
Except for exterior basement walls or retaining walls, bearing or shear walls more than 210 mm thick
shall have the reinforcement for each direction placed in two layers. Each layer shall be placed not more
than t/3 from the surface of the wall.

Section 14.1.8.4 - Spacing of reinforcement (Starts on Page 133)
The vertical and horizontal reinforcement in each layer shall not be spaced farther apart than three
times the wall thickness or 500 mm, whichever is less.

Section 14.1.8.5 - Distributed vertical reinforcement (Starts on Page 133)
The minimum area of distributed vertical reinforcement between boundary elements shall be 0.0015Ag.

Section 14.1.8.6 - Distributed horizontal reinforcement (Starts on Page 133)
The minimum area of distributed horizontal reinforcement shall be 0.002Ag. However, where crack
control is critical or wall geometry or the length of the wall between joints causes significant restraint of
shrinkage or thermal strains, reinforcement additional to that specified in this Clause, or other crack
control measures, shall be considered.

Section 14.1.8.7 - Ties for distributed vertical compression reinforcement (Starts on Page 133)
Distributed vertical reinforcement required as compression reinforcement shall be tied and detailed in
accordance with the requirements for column reinforcement specified in Clause 7, except that ties may
be omitted if
a)
the area of vertical steel is less than 0.005Ag; and
b)
the bar size is 20M or smaller.

Section 14.1.8.8.1 - Nominal concentrated vertical reinforcement (Starts on Page 133)
Concentrated vertical reinforcement consisting of not fewer than two 15M vertical bars shall be
provided at each end of all walls.

Section 14.1.8.8.2 - Reinforcement for flexure (Starts on Page 133)
Concentrated vertical reinforcement shall be provided in boundary elements of shear walls to provide
that part of the resistance to strong-axis bending not provided by the reinforcement specified in
Clause 14.1.8.5.

Section 14.1.8.8.3 - Concentrated vertical reinforcement limits (Starts on Page 133)
The reinforcement ratio within any region of concentrated reinforcement, including regions containing
lap splices, shall be not more than 0.08.

Section 14.1.8.8.4 - Ties for concentrated vertical reinforcement (Starts on Page 133)
Concentrated vertical reinforcement in excess of two 20M bars shall be tied and detailed as specified in
Clause 7.
reproduction interdite.

Section 14.1.8.9 - Reinforcement at openings (Starts on Page 134)
In addition to the reinforcement required by Clauses 14.1.8.5, 14.1.8.6, and 14.1.8.8.1, not less than
one 15M bar per layer, or reinforcement having the same area, shall be provided around all window and
door or similar openings. Such bars shall extend to develop the bar, but not less than 600 mm beyond
each corner of the opening.

Section 14.2.1 - Except as permitted by Clause 14.2.2, bearing walls shall be designed as specified in Clauses 7, 10, and (Starts on Page 134)
11.

Section 14.2.2.2 - Clause 14.2.2.1 shall apply only if the following requirements are met: (Starts on Page 134)
a)
the wall has a solid rectangular cross-section that is constant over the height of the wall;
b)
the principal moments act about a horizontal axis parallel to the plane of the wall;
c)
the resultant of all factored axial loads, including the effects of the principal moment, is located
within the middle third of the overall wall thickness; and
d)
the wall is supported against lateral displacement along at least the top and bottom edges.

Section 1.0 - 14.2.3 (Starts on Page 134)
If present, the calculation of the factored resistance of bearing walls shall account for significant strong
axis bending moments in accordance with Clause 10.
Note: Strong axis bending moments may be applied to bearing walls due to the resultant of the axial load not
being at the centroid of the section due to in-plane offset of the wall or may be induced by deformation of the
lateral force resisting system subjected to the factored lateral loads such as wind and seismic loads.

Section 14.3 - Structural design of non-bearing walls (Starts on Page 134)
Non-bearing walls, including retaining walls and transversely loaded walls, shall be designed in
accordance with the provisions of Clauses 10 and 11.
reproduction interdite.

Section 14.4.1 - General (Starts on Page 135)
In addition to the requirements specified elsewhere in Clause 14, the following shall apply to shear
walls:
a)
flexural shear walls shall be designed for factored axial load, factored moment about one or both
axes, and factored shear as specified in Clauses 10 and 11; and
b)
squat shear walls may be designed using strut-and-tie models in accordance with Clause 11.4 and
the applicable provisions of Clause 14.1.

Section 14.4.2.1 - General (Starts on Page 135)
The design of flexural shear walls shall satisfy either Clause 14.4.2.2 or Clause 14.4.2.3.

Section 14.4.2.2 - Low axial compression (Starts on Page 135)
When the factored axial compression applied to a wall is such that the tension reinforcement will reach
yield in accordance with Clause 10.5.2, the compression end of the wall shall have a wall thickness of at
least hu/20 unless
a)
the compression strain depth, c, is smaller than the lesser of 4bw or 0.3‚Ñìw or;
b)
a continuous line of lateral support is provided to the compression end of the wall by a cross wall
or wall flange having a width not less than hu/5.

Section 14.4.2.3 - High axial compression (Starts on Page 135)
When the factored axial compression applied to a wall is such that the tension reinforcement will not
reach yield in accordance with Clause 10.5.2, the design of the wall shall consider slenderness effects.
However, the influence of wall slenderness need not apply to any part of a wall that lies within a
distance of 3bw from a continuous line of lateral support provided by a cross wall or a flange having a
width not less than hu/5.
The required thickness of a wall subjected to the factored axial compression and moment about the
strong axis may be determined using a reduced length of wall. The reduced length of wall shall be such
that the resultant axial force representing axial load and moment acts at the centre of the reduced
length. The thickness of the wall shall satisfy the slenderness requirements of Clause 10.15 for the
factored axial load applied to the reduced length of wall.

Section 14.4.3.1 - Shear connection (Starts on Page 135)
In assemblies of interconnected shear walls designed to act as a unit, reinforcement shall be provided
to transmit the shear stresses necessary for the assembly of interconnected walls to act as a unit.

Section 14.4.3.2 - Compression flanges of assemblies of interconnected shear walls (Starts on Page 135)
If a compression flange of an assembly of interconnected shear walls has a thickness less than hc/15, or
less than wc/15 if adjacent shear wall webs are present, the factored axial and moment resistances of
the wall assembly shall both be multiplied by
üìê Equation 14.2
where ‚Ñìc is the lesser of wc and hc and œâ shall not be less than 0.75 or greater than 1.0.
reproduction interdite.

Section 14.4.3.3 - Maximum widths of overhanging flanges (Starts on Page 136)
The effective widths of overhanging flanges of walls shall not be assumed to extend farther from the
face of the web than the smaller of
a)
half the clear distance to an adjacent shear wall web; or
b)
25% of the total wall height above the section under consideration.

Section 14.4.4 - Horizontal reinforcement in shear walls (Starts on Page 136)
Horizontal reinforcement shall extend to the ends of the wall with horizontal reinforcement required
for shear to be anchored in accordance with Clause 12.13.2 or anchored within regions of concentrated
reinforcement at the ends of the wall.

Section 14.4.5 - Weak axis bending (Starts on Page 136)
Weak axis bending of shear walls shall be considered in conjunction with strong axis bending.

Section 14.4.6 - Diaphragms (Starts on Page 136)
Floor and roof diaphragms shall be designed to transfer lateral forces between walls or other lateral-
load-resisting elements and floor and roof diaphragms. Connections between the diaphragms and the
frames or other lateral-load-resisting elements shall be designed to resist the forces that are
transferred.

Section 14.4.7 - Coupling beams (Starts on Page 136)
The diameter of flexural reinforcing bars in coupling beams shall be selected to provide a development
length not more than one-half of the clear beam span. Alternatively, diagonal reinforcement shall be
provided as specified in Clause 21.5.8.2. However, ties satisfying the requirements specified in
Clause 7.6 may be provided in lieu of hoops or spirals.
15 Foundations

Section 15.1 - General (Starts on Page 136)
Clauses 15.2 to 15.12 shall apply to the design of isolated footings and, where applicable, to combined
footings, mats, and deep foundations.

Section 15.2.2 - The base area of the footing or the number and arrangement of piles shall be selected based on the (Starts on Page 136)
principles of soil mechanics. Where the analysis of footings is based on other than linear distributions of
soil pressure, the assumed distributions shall be based on an analysis of the interaction of the soil and
the footing in accordance with the stiffness of both elements.
reproduction interdite.

Section 15.2.3 - Piles and pile caps in deep foundations shall be designed on the assumption that each axial pile reaction (Starts on Page 137)
acts at an eccentricity, in any direction, equal to the specified pile location tolerance, but not less than
50 mm.

Section 15.3 - Footings and pile caps supporting circular or regular polygonal columns or (Starts on Page 137)
pedestals
In lieu of detailed analysis, circular or regular polygonal concrete columns or pedestals may be treated
as square members, with the same area, for the location of critical sections for moment, shear, and
development of reinforcement in the footings or pile caps.

Section 15.4.1 - Design for flexure shall meet the requirements of Clause 10. (Starts on Page 137)
Note: For many types of footings, the strut-and-tie method specified in Clause 11.4 can be used for design.

Section 15.4.2 - The external moment on any section of a footing shall be determined by passing a vertical plane (Starts on Page 137)
through the footing and computing the moment of the forces acting over the entire area of the footing
on one side of that vertical plane.

Section 15.4.3 - The maximum factored moment for an isolated footing shall be computed at the critical sections (Starts on Page 137)
located as follows:
a)
for footings supporting a concrete column, pedestal, or wall: at the face of the column, pedestal, or
wall;
b)
for footings supporting a masonry wall: halfway between the middle and the edge of the wall; and
c)
for footings supporting a column with steel base plates: as determined by considering the
dimensions and the stiffness of the base plate.
Note: In many cases, the critical section can be taken halfway between the face of the column and the edge of the
base plate.

Section 15.4.4 - 15.4.4.1 (Starts on Page 137)
In two-way rectangular footings, reinforcement shall be distributed as follows:
a)
reinforcement in the long direction shall be distributed uniformly across the entire width of the
footing; and
b)
for reinforcement in the short direction, a portion of the total reinforcement specified in Clause

Section 15.4.4.2 - shall be distributed uniformly over a band width (centred on the centreline of the column (Starts on Page 137)
or pedestal) equal to the length of the short side of the footing or equal to the length of the
supported wall or column, whichever is greater. The remainder of the reinforcement required in
the short direction shall be distributed uniformly outside the centre band width.

Section 15.5.1 - Design for shear shall meet the requirements of Clauses 11 and 13. (Starts on Page 138)
Note: For many types of footings, the strut-and-tie method specified in Clause 11.4 can be used for design.

Section 15.5.2 - The location of the critical section for shear, as specified in Clause 13.3, shall be measured from the face (Starts on Page 138)
of the column, pedestal, or wall for footings supporting a column, pedestal, or wall. For footings
supporting a column or pedestal with steel base plates, the critical section shall be measured from the
location specified in Clause 15.4.3 c).

Section 15.5.3 - Shear on any section through a pile cap shall be computed in accordance with the following: (Starts on Page 138)
a)
the entire reaction from any pile whose centre is located dp/2 or more outside the section shall be
considered as producing shear on that section;
b)
the reaction from any pile whose centre is located dp/2 or more inside the section shall be
considered as producing no shear on that section; and
c)
for intermediate positions of the pile centre, the portion of the pile reaction to be considered as
producing shear on the section shall be based on a straight-line interpolation between the full
value at dp/2 outside the section and zero value at dp/2 inside the section.

Section 15.6.1 - The development of reinforcement in footings and pile caps shall be computed in accordance with (Starts on Page 138)
Clause 12.

Section 15.6.2 - When the strut-and-tie method is used, the development of tension reinforcement shall be as specified (Starts on Page 138)
in Clause 11.4.3.2.

Section 15.6.3 - The critical sections for development of reinforcement shall be assumed to be at the locations specified (Starts on Page 138)
in Clause 15.4.3 for the maximum factored moment and at all other vertical planes where changes of
section or reinforcement occur. See also Clause 12.10.5.

Section 15.7 - Minimum depth of footings (Starts on Page 138)
The depth of footings above the bottom reinforcement shall be not less than 150 mm.
reproduction interdite.

Section 15.8.1 - Design of piles (Starts on Page 139)
The moments and shears in the piles caused by lateral loads shall be calculated using procedures that
account for the pile-soil interaction and non-linear soil behaviour.

Section 15.8.2.1 - The stability of portions of piles without lateral restraint from soil shall be assessed as specified in (Starts on Page 139)
Clause 10.

Section 15.8.2.2 - The outer 25 mm concrete layer of uncased drilled piles shall be neglected when the factored resistance (Starts on Page 139)
of the pile shaft and the end-bearing resistance is determined.

Section 15.8.2.3 - For uncased drilled piles, a reduction factor of 0.90 shall be applied to the factored resistance specified (Starts on Page 139)
in Clauses 10 and 11.

Section 15.8.2.4 - Selection of the pile bell diameter and bell side slope shall be based on the concrete shear resistance (Starts on Page 139)
and the type of the soil (see ACI 336.3R).

Section 15.8.3 - Minimum depth of pile caps (Starts on Page 139)
The depth of pile caps above the bottom reinforcement and above the top of the pile shall be not less
than 300 mm.

Section 15.9.1.1 - The forces and moments at the base of a column, pile cap, wall, or pedestal shall be transferred to the (Starts on Page 139)
supporting footing or pile.

Section 15.9.1.2 - Bearing on concrete at the contact surface between the supported and supporting members shall not (Starts on Page 139)
exceed the factored bearing resistance of either member specified in Clause 10.8.
reproduction interdite.

Section 15.9.1.3 - Reinforcement, dowels, or mechanical connectors between supported and supporting members shall be (Starts on Page 140)
adequate to transfer
a)
all compressive force that exceeds the concrete bearing strength of either member; and
b)
any computed tensile force across the interface.
In addition, reinforcement, dowels, or mechanical connectors shall meet the requirements of Clause 12
and of Clause 15.9.2.2 or 15.9.2.3.

Section 15.9.1.4 - Lateral forces shall be transferred to the supporting pedestals, caps, piles, and footings in accordance (Starts on Page 140)
with the interface shear transfer requirements of Clause 11.5 or by other appropriate means.

Section 15.9.2.1 - For columns, pile caps, piles, and pedestals, the area of reinforcement across the interface shall be not (Starts on Page 140)
less than 0.005 times the gross area of the supported member.

Section 15.9.2.2 - For cast-in-place walls, the area of reinforcement across the interface shall be not less than the (Starts on Page 140)
minimum vertical reinforcement required by Clause 14.1.8.

Section 15.9.2.4 - At footings, 45M and 55M longitudinal bars (in compression only) may be lap spliced with dowels to (Starts on Page 140)
provide the reinforcement required to satisfy Clause 15.9.1. Dowels shall not be larger than 35M and
shall extend into the supported member for a distance of not less than the development length of 45M
or 55M bars or the splice length of the dowels, whichever is greater, and into the footing for a distance
of not less than the development length of the dowels.

Section 15.9.2.5 - If a pinned or rocker connection is provided in cast-in-place construction, the connection shall also (Starts on Page 140)
comply with Clause 15.9.3.

Section 15.9.3.1 - In precast concrete construction, the reinforcement required to satisfy Clause 15.9.1 may be provided (Starts on Page 140)
by anchor bolts or suitable mechanical connectors.

Section 15.9.3.2 - Anchor bolts and mechanical connectors shall be designed to reach their factored resistance prior to (Starts on Page 140)
anchorage failure of the surrounding concrete.

reproduction interdite.

Section 15.10.1 - In sloped or stepped footings, the angle of the slope or the depth and location of the steps shall be such (Starts on Page 141)

16 Precast concrete

Section 16.1.1 - All requirements of this Standard not specifically excluded and not in conflict with the requirements of (Starts on Page 141)
Clauses 16.1.2 to 16.5.3.7 shall apply to structures incorporating precast concrete elements.

concrete manufacturer.

material resistance factor, œïc, specified in Clause 8.4.2 of this Standard may be taken as 0.70.

accordance with CSA A23.4.
reproduction interdite.

Section 16.2.2 - Exemptions to the requirements specified in Clause 16.2.1 may be made by the designer of the building (Starts on Page 142)
for the following reinforced concrete elements:
a)

b)

accordance with CSA A23.4, in which case certification shall be required, or in accordance with CSA

Section 16.3 - Drawings (Starts on Page 142)
In addition to the requirements specified in Clause 5, drawings and related documents shall include the
following:
a)
sufficient dimensions to permit preparation of the shop drawings;
b)
sufficient indication of the work supporting, supported by, or attached to the precast concrete to
permit preparation of the shop drawings;
c)
the class of surface finish required for structural purposes;
d)
any non-standard tolerances required for the precast concrete elements or the building structure;
e)
any superimposed loads on the precast concrete elements, the location of connections, and the
factored forces to be developed at the connections to the elements;
f)
when precast elements are to act as diaphragms, the factored external forces and shears acting on
the diaphragms; and
g)
the expected deformations of the structure under specified loads, insofar as they affect the design
of the precast concrete elements or associated connections. Deformations due to specified
earthquake loads shall be shown separately.

Section 16.4.1.1 - The design shall take into account loading and restraint conditions from the initial fabrication to the (Starts on Page 142)
intended use of the structure, including forces from stripping, storage, transportation, and erection.

interconnected elements.

creep, and shrinkage.

to the lateral load resisting system, as specified in Clause 16.4.2.2 b).

Section 16.5.2.3 - Where precast elements form floor or roof diaphragms, the connections between the diaphragm and (Starts on Page 145)
those elements being laterally supported shall be designed for all factored loads but shall have a
factored tensile resistance of not less than 5 kN/m.

Section 16.5.2.4 - Vertical tension tie requirements shall apply to the horizontal joints in all vertical structural elements, (Starts on Page 145)
except cladding, and shall meet the following requirements:
a)
precast columns shall have a factored tensile resistance of not less than 1.4Ag N;
b)
for columns with a larger cross-section than required by analysis, a reduced effective area may be
substituted for Ag, but it shall be not less than Ag/2; and
c)
precast wall panels shall have a minimum of two ties per panel, with a factored resistance of not
less than 30 kN per tie.

Section 16.5.2.6 - Ties and connections shall be designed in such a manner that the resistance is governed by yielding of (Starts on Page 145)
the steel component.

Section 16.5.3 - 16.5.3.1 (Starts on Page 145)
Structures that are three or more storeys high and are constructed with precast concrete bearing walls
shall be tied together as specified in Clauses 16.5.3.2 to 16.5.3.5.

Section 16.5.3.2 - Tension ties shall be incorporated in floor and roof systems to provide a factored resistance of not less (Starts on Page 145)
than 14 kN per metre of horizontal wall length for longitudinal ties and 14 kN per metre of floor or roof
span for transverse ties. Tie paths shall be provided over interior wall supports and to exterior walls.
Ties shall be located in the floor or roof system or within 600 mm of the plane of the floor or roof
system.

Section 16.5.3.3 - Longitudinal tension ties parallel to the floor or roof spans shall be spaced not more than 3000 mm on (Starts on Page 145)
centres. Provisions shall be made to transfer forces around openings.
reproduction interdite.

Section 16.5.3.4 - Transverse tension ties perpendicular to the span of the floor or roof shall be spaced at a distance not (Starts on Page 146)
greater than the distance between the bearing walls.

Section 16.5.3.5 - Tension ties around the perimeter of each floor and roof, within 1500 mm of the edge, shall provide a (Starts on Page 146)
factored tensile resistance of not less than 60 kN.

Section 16.5.3.6 - Vertical tension ties shall be provided in all walls and shall be continuous over the full height of the (Starts on Page 146)
building. They shall provide a factored tensile resistance of not less than 40 kN per metre of horizontal
wall length. Not fewer than two tension ties shall be provided for each precast wall panel.

Section 16.5.3.7 - During checking for structural integrity, any beneficial effects of friction caused by gravity loads shall not (Starts on Page 146)
be considered for the transfer of horizontal loads.
17 Composite concrete flexural members
Note: This Clause uses the terms ‚Äútransverse shear‚Äú and ‚Äúlongitudinal shear‚Äù. For a composite beam with a
horizontal axis, ‚Äútransverse shear‚Äù refers to vertical shear forces and ‚Äúlongitudinal shear‚Äù refers to shear on a
horizontal plane.

Section 17.1.1 - Clauses 17.1.2 to 17.5.4 shall apply to the design of composite concrete flexural members consisting of (Starts on Page 146)
concrete elements constructed in separate placements, but interconnected in such a manner that all
elements act as a unit.

Section 17.1.2 - All of the requirements of this Standard shall apply to composite flexural members, except where (Starts on Page 146)
modified by Clauses 17.1.3 to 17.5.4.

Section 17.1.5 - If the specified strength, density, or other properties of the elements differ, the properties of the (Starts on Page 146)
individual elements shall be used for the analysis.
Note: Differential creep and shrinkage can affect the distributions of strains and deformations in the individual
elements.
reproduction interdite.

Section 17.1.6 - In strength computations for composite members, no distinction shall be made between shored and (Starts on Page 147)
unshored members.

Section 17.1.7 - All elements shall be designed to support all loads introduced prior to full development of the design (Starts on Page 147)
strength of composite members.

Section 17.1.8 - Reinforcement shall be provided, as necessary, to control cracking and prevent separation of individual (Starts on Page 147)
elements of composite members.

Section 17.2 - Shoring (Starts on Page 147)
When used, shoring shall not be removed until the supported elements have developed the design
properties required to support all loads and to limit deflections and cracking at the time of shoring
removal.

Section 17.3.1 - When an entire composite member is assumed to resist transverse shear, the design shall meet the (Starts on Page 147)
requirements specified in Clause 11 for a monolithically cast member of the same cross-sectional shape.

Section 17.4.1 - For a composite member, steps shall be taken to ensure full transfer of the longitudinal shear forces at (Starts on Page 147)
the contact surfaces of the interconnected elements.

Section 17.4.3 - 17.4.3.1 (Starts on Page 147)

shall be based on

Section 17.4.4 - Longitudinal shear may be investigated by computing the actual compressive or tensile force in any (Starts on Page 148)

Section 17.4.5 - When tension exists across any contact surface between interconnected elements, shear transfer by (Starts on Page 148)

Clause 11.2.8, and the tie spacing shall not exceed four times the least dimension of the supported
element or 600 mm, whichever is less.

Section 17.5.2 - Ties for longitudinal shear shall consist of a single bar or wire, multiple leg stirrups, vertical legs, or (Starts on Page 148)
welded wire fabric (smooth or deformed).

Section 17.5.4 - Reinforcement for transverse shear that is anchored into the interconnected elements as specified in (Starts on Page 148)

18 Prestressed concrete

Section 18.1.1 - Clauses 18.1.2 to 18.13.4 shall apply to members prestressed with wires, strands, or bars that comply (Starts on Page 149)
with the requirements for prestressing steels specified in Clause 4.1.4 and in CSA A23.1.
Note: Unbonded tendons are more susceptible to corrosion than bonded tendons. The durability of structures with
unbonded prestressing tendons is a function of the environment, occupancy type, and quality of work during
construction. Ingress of moisture or chlorides, sulphides, nitrates, carbonates, or other industrial, food processing,
or agricultural chemicals can cause corrosion or even failure of the tendons. Water, including rainwater, can enter
the sheath during tendon shipping, storage, or construction, and in some cases after occupancy of the structure, if
adequate protection is not provided. Materials and quality of work should meet the requirements specified in
CSA A23.1.

Section 18.1.2 - All of the requirements of this Standard not specifically excluded and not in conflict with the (Starts on Page 149)
requirements specified in Clauses 18.1.1 and 18.1.3 to 18.13.4 shall apply to prestressed concrete.

Section 18.1.5 - The effects of the loads at all loading stages that could be critical during the life of the member from (Starts on Page 149)
the time the prestress is first applied shall be considered.

Section 18.1.6 - The stresses in prestressed members at transfer and under specified loads shall satisfy the requirements (Starts on Page 149)
of Clause 18.3.

Section 18.1.7 - Stress concentrations due to prestressing shall be considered. Adequately anchored transverse (Starts on Page 149)
reinforcement shall be provided to control splitting.

Section 18.1.9 - When adjoining parts of the structure can restrain the elastic and long-term deformations (deflections, (Starts on Page 149)
changes in length, and rotation) of a member caused by prestressing, applied loading, foundation
settlement, temperature, and shrinkage, the restraint shall be estimated and its effects on the member
and on the restraining structure shall be considered.
reproduction interdite.

Section 18.1.10 - The possibility of buckling in a member between points where concrete and prestressing tendons are in (Starts on Page 150)
contact and of buckling in thin webs and flanges shall be considered.

Section 18.2.1 - The design of prestressed members for flexure and axial loads shall be based on the assumptions (Starts on Page 150)
specified in Clause 10.1.

Section 18.2.2 - For investigation of the stress limits specified in Clauses 18.3 and 18.4, linear elastic material behaviour (Starts on Page 150)
may be assumed. Concrete may be assumed to resist tension at sections that are uncracked.

Section 18.3.1 - 18.3.1.1 (Starts on Page 150)
Stresses in concrete immediately after prestress transfer due to prestress, and the specified loads
present at transfer, shall not exceed the following:
a) Extreme fibre stress in compression except as

supported members

Section 18.3.1.2 - The stress specified in Clause 18.3.1.1 a) may be exceeded if tests or analyses demonstrate that (Starts on Page 150)
performance will not be impaired.

Section 18.3.1.3 - Where computed tensile stresses exceed the values specified in Items b) and c) of Clause 18.3.1.1, (Starts on Page 150)
bonded reinforcement with a minimum area of As = Nc/(0.5fy) shall be provided in the tensile zone to
resist the total tensile force, Nc, in the concrete computed on the basis of an uncracked section.

Section 18.3.3 - 18.3.3.1 (Starts on Page 151)
Partially prestressed members may exceed the requirements specified in Clause 18.3.2 c) provided that
tests or analyses demonstrate adequate fatigue resistance as well as adequate deflection and crack
control under specified loads.

Section 18.3.3.2 - Partially prestressed members not subjected to fatigue conditions and not exposed to a corrosive (Starts on Page 151)
environment may be deemed to have adequate deflection and crack control if the requirements of
Clauses 9.8.4 and 18.8 are met.

Section 18.4 - Permissible stresses in tendons (Starts on Page 151)
Tensile stress in tendons shall not exceed the following:
Stress due to tendon jacking force for post-tensioning tendons
0.85fpu, but not greater than 0.94fpy
Stress due to tendon jacking force for pretensioning tendons
0.80fpu
Stress immediately after prestress transfer
0.82fpy, but not greater than 0.74fpu
Stress in post-tensioning tendons at anchorages and couplers
immediately after tendon anchorage
0.70fpu
However, the stress due to tendon jacking force for post-tensioning and pretensioning tendons shall not
exceed the maximum value recommended by the manufacturer of the prestressing tendons or
anchorages. If pretensioned tendons are subjected to a temperature drop prior to concreting, the stress
at the reduced temperatures shall not exceed 0.80 fpu.
Note: The specified yield strength of prestressing tendons is based on the requirements specified in ASTM A 416/A
416M, ASTM A 421/A 421M, and ASTM A 722/A 722M, which specify the following minimum values for fpy:
a)
low relaxation strand or wire: 0.90fpu;
b)
stress-relieved strand or wire: 0.85fpu;
c)
plain prestressing bars: 0.85fpu; and
d)
deformed prestressing bars: 0.80fpu.

anchorage seating loss;
b)
elastic shortening of concrete;
reproduction interdite.

friction loss due to intended and unintended curvature in post-tensioning tendons;
d)
creep of concrete;
e)
shrinkage of concrete; and
f)
relaxation of tendon stress.

Moments for computing the required strength shall be the sum of the moments due to reactions

Section 18.11.1 - General (Starts on Page 154)
The design of prestressed concrete members subject to combined flexure and axial loads shall be based
on Clauses 10.9 to 10.16. The effects of prestress, creep, shrinkage, and temperature change shall be
included.

Section 18.11.2.1 - Members with average prestress, fcp, less than 1.5 MPa shall have the minimum reinforcement specified (Starts on Page 154)
in Clauses 7.6 and 10.9 for columns or Clause 14.1.8 for walls.

Section 18.11.2.2 - Except for walls, members with average prestress, fcp, equal to or greater than 1.5 MPa shall have all of (Starts on Page 154)
their prestressing tendons enclosed by spirals or lateral ties as follows:

b)

Factored moments and shears in prestressed slab systems reinforced for flexure in two directions shall

Section 18.12.2.1 - When Clause 13.8 is used, flexural stresses due to unfactored gravity loads in column strips shall be (Starts on Page 155)
determined by taking 75% of interior negative moments, 100% of exterior negative moments, and 60%
of positive moments unless a more detailed analysis is performed.

Section 18.12.2.2 - Concrete stresses due to prestressing may be assumed to be uniformly distributed across the slab (Starts on Page 155)
unless a more detailed analysis is performed.

reinforcement shall not exceed 300 mm.

thickness or 1500 mm unless adequate additional bonded reinforcement is provided so that the slab
has the strength to span between tendons.

each column. These tendons or bars shall satisfy the requirements specified in Clause 13.10.6.

prestressing forces.

Section 18.13.4 - Regions of stress concentrations due to abrupt changes in section or other causes shall be adequately (Starts on Page 157)
reinforced.

Section 18.13.5 - Three dimensional effects shall be considered in design and analyzed using three-dimensional (Starts on Page 157)
procedures or approximated by considering the summation of effects for two orthogonal planes.
19 Shells and folded plates

Section 19.1.1 - Clauses 19.1.2 to 19.5.2 shall apply to thin shell and folded plate concrete structures, including ribs and (Starts on Page 157)
edge members.

Section 19.1.2 - All of the requirements of this Standard not specifically excluded and not in conflict with the (Starts on Page 157)
requirements of Clauses 19.1 and 19.2 to 19.5.2 shall apply to thin shell structures.

Section 19.2.1 - Elastic behaviour shall be an acceptable basis for determining internal forces and displacements of thin (Starts on Page 157)
shells. This behaviour may be established by computations based on an analysis of the uncracked
concrete structure in which the material is assumed to be linearly elastic, homogeneous, and isotropic.
Poisson's ratio of concrete may be assumed to be equal to zero.
Note: See Clause 13.7 for further guidance on analysis and design.

Section 19.2.2 - Equilibrium checks of internal resistances and external loads shall be conducted to ensure consistency (Starts on Page 157)
of results.

Section 19.2.3 - Experimental or numerical analysis procedures shall be used only when it can be shown that they (Starts on Page 157)
provide a safe basis for design.

Section 19.2.4 - Approximate methods of analysis not satisfying compatibility of strains either within the shell or (Starts on Page 157)
between the shell and auxiliary members shall be used only when it can be shown that they provide a
safe basis for design.
reproduction interdite.

Section 19.2.5 - For prestressed shells, the analysis shall also consider behaviour under loads induced during (Starts on Page 158)
prestressing, at cracking load, and at factored load. Where prestressing tendons are draped within a
shell, the design shall take into account the force components on the shell resulting from the tendon
profiles not lying in one plane.

Section 19.2.6 - The thickness, h, of a thin shell and its reinforcement shall be proportioned for the required strength (Starts on Page 158)
and serviceability.

Section 19.2.8 - Auxiliary members shall be designed in accordance with the applicable requirements of this Standard. A (Starts on Page 158)
portion of the shell equal to the flange width specified in Clause 10.3 may be assumed to act with the
auxiliary member. In such portions of the shell, the reinforcement perpendicular to the auxiliary
member shall be at least equal to that required for the flange of a T-beam by Clause 10.5.3.2.

Section 19.3 - Specified yield strength of reinforcement (Starts on Page 158)
For non-prestressed reinforcement, the yield strength used in calculations shall not exceed 400 MPa.

Section 19.4.1 - Shell reinforcement shall be provided to resist tensile stresses from the internal membrane forces, to (Starts on Page 158)
resist bending and twisting moments, to control shrinkage and temperature cracking, and as special
reinforcement at shell boundaries, load attachments, and shell openings.

Section 19.4.3 - The area of shell reinforcement in two orthogonal directions at any section shall be not less than the (Starts on Page 158)
minimum slab reinforcement required by Clause 7.8, except as specified in Clause 19.4.7.

Section 19.4.4 - The reinforcement necessary for resisting shell membrane forces shall be provided so that the factored (Starts on Page 158)
resistance in any direction shall be at least equal to the component of the principal membrane forces in
the same direction due to the factored loads.

Section 19.4.5 - The area of shell tension reinforcement shall be limited so that the reinforcement will yield before (Starts on Page 158)
crushing of concrete in compression can take place.
reproduction interdite.

Section 19.4.6 - In regions of high tension, membrane reinforcement shall, if practical, be placed in the general (Starts on Page 159)
directions of the principal tensile membrane forces. Where this is not practical, membrane
reinforcement may be placed in two or more directions.
Note: If the direction of reinforcement varies more than 15¬∞ from the direction of principal tensile membrane force,
it is possible that the amount of reinforcement will have to be increased to limit the width of possible cracks under
specified loads.

Section 19.4.7 - When the magnitude of the principal tensile membrane stress within the shell varies greatly over the (Starts on Page 159)
area of the shell surface, reinforcement resisting the total tension may be concentrated in the regions
of largest tensile stress if it can be shown that this provides a safe basis for design. However, the ratio of
shell reinforcement in any portion of the tensile zone shall be not less than 0.0035 based on the overall
thickness of the shell.

Section 19.4.8 - Reinforcement required to resist shell bending moments shall be proportioned with due regard for the (Starts on Page 159)
simultaneous action of membrane axial forces at the same location. When shell reinforcement is
required in only one face to resist bending moments, equal amounts shall be placed near both surfaces
of the shell, even if calculations do not indicate reversal of bending moments.

Section 19.4.9 - When splitting of the shell near its mid-thickness can occur because of transverse tensile stresses, (Starts on Page 159)
transverse reinforcement shall be provided to prevent the cracks from propagating.

Section 19.4.10 - Shell reinforcement in any direction shall not be spaced farther apart than 500 mm or five times the (Starts on Page 159)
shell thickness. Where the principal membrane tensile stress on the gross concrete area due to factored
loads exceeds
, reinforcement shall not be spaced farther apart than three times the shell
thickness.

Section 19.4.11 - Shell reinforcement at the junction of the shell and supporting members or edge members shall be (Starts on Page 159)
anchored in or extended through such members in accordance with Clause 12, except that the
minimum development length shall be 1.2‚Ñìd but not less than 500 mm.

Section 19.4.12 - Splice development lengths of shell reinforcement shall meet the requirements of Clause 12, except (Starts on Page 159)
that the minimum splice length of tension bars shall be 1.2 times the value specified in Clause 12 but
not less than 500 mm. The number of splices in principal tensile reinforcement shall be kept to a
practical minimum. Where splices are necessary, they shall be staggered at least ‚Ñìd, with not more than
one-third of the reinforcement spliced at any section.

Section 19.5.1 - When removal of formwork is based on a specific modulus of elasticity of concrete because of stability (Starts on Page 159)
or deflection considerations, the value of the modulus of elasticity, Ec, shall be determined from flexural
reproduction interdite.
tests of field-cured beam specimens. The number and dimensions of the test beam specimens, and the
test procedures, shall be specified by the designer.
Note: For guidance see CSA A23.2-3C.

Section 19.5.2 - If a thin shell is constructed with deviations from the shape greater than the tolerances specified by the (Starts on Page 160)
designer, an analysis of the effect of such deviations shall be conducted and all necessary remedial
actions shall be taken to ensure the shell‚Äôs safe behaviour.
20 Strength evaluation procedures
Notes:
1)
This Clause specifies requirements and procedures for evaluating the strength or safe load rating of structures
or structural elements where
a)
doubt exists about their adequacy because of apparent or suspected deficiencies or defects;
b)
the strength or load-bearing capacity is unknown;
c)
a change of function creates loading characteristics different from those provided for in the design of
the structure; or
d)
damage that has possibility reduced the strength or load-bearing capacity has occurred.
2)
If the structure under investigation does not meet the requirements specified in Clause 20.2.3, 20.3.1.10, or
20.3.2.1, a lower load rating for the structure based on the results of the load test or analysis may be
assigned.

Section 20.1 - General (Starts on Page 160)
When the safety of a structure or structural member is in doubt and a structural strength investigation
is necessary, it shall be carried out by analysis, by means of load tests, or by a combination of these
methods.

Section 20.2.1 - If the strength evaluation is performed by analytical means, a thorough field investigation of the (Starts on Page 160)
dimensions and details of the members as actually built, of the properties of the materials, and of other
pertinent conditions of the existing structure shall be conducted.

Section 20.2.2 - If drawings or other documents are used in the evaluation specified in Clause 20.2.1, their completeness (Starts on Page 160)
and any modifications of the structure not reflected on the drawings shall be considered in the
evaluation.

Section 20.2.3 - The analysis based on the investigation specified in Clause 20.2.1 shall satisfy the requirements of this (Starts on Page 160)
Standard.

Section 20.3 - Load tests (Starts on Page 160)
Notes:
1)
Although load tests should be conducted in a manner that will provide for safety of life and structure, safety
measures should not interfere with the load test procedures or affect results.
reproduction interdite.
2)
Load testing prestressed systems with unbonded tendons where corrosion is suspected is generally not an
acceptable method for evaluating such tendons.

Section 20.3.1.1 - Before conducting a load test in accordance with this Clause, calculations shall be made to ensure that (Starts on Page 161)
the expected failure mode is ductile. If the expected failure mode is brittle, load testing using the
provisions of this Clause shall not be carried out.
Note: The provisions of Clause 20 are based on deflection recovery of systems or members with capacities that are
governed by ductile flexural failure. A load test in accordance with Clause 20 is inappropriate for assessing the
reliability of a member with a brittle failure mode.

Section 20.3.1.2 - If the strength evaluation is based on load tests, an engineer experienced in such evaluations shall (Starts on Page 161)
control the tests.

Section 20.3.1.3 - A load test shall generally not be conducted until the portion of the structure subjected to load is at (Starts on Page 161)
least 28 days old.
Note: When the owner of the structure, the contractor, and all other involved parties mutually agree, the test may
be conducted when the structure is less than 28 days old.

Section 20.3.1.4 - The structure or portion of the structure to be load tested shall be loaded in such a manner as to test (Starts on Page 161)
adequately the suspected weakness and to allow for the characteristics and pattern of the expected
loads.

Section 20.3.1.5 - A load to simulate the effect of the portion of the dead loads not already present shall be applied 24 h (Starts on Page 161)
before application of the test load and shall remain in place until all testing has been completed.

Section 20.3.1.6 - The superimposed test load shall be applied in not fewer than four approximately equal increments (Starts on Page 161)
without shock to the structure and in a manner that avoids arching of the load materials.

Section 20.3.1.7 - When an entire structural system in doubt is load tested or an entire questionable portion of a system is (Starts on Page 161)
load tested, the test load shall be 90% of the factored loads Mf, Vf, and Pf.

Section 20.3.1.8 - When only a portion of a structural system in doubt is tested and the results of the tests are taken as (Starts on Page 161)
representative of the structural adequacy of untested portions of the system, the test load shall be
equal to the factored loads Mf, Vf, and Pf.

Section 20.3.1.9 - The test load shall be left on the structure for 24 h. (Starts on Page 161)
reproduction interdite.

Section 20.3.1.10 - If the portion of the structure tested fails or shows visible indications of impending failure, it shall be (Starts on Page 162)
considered to have failed the test.

Section 20.3.2.1 - Note: The requirements of this Clause are in addition to the requirements specified in Clause 20.3.1.10. (Starts on Page 162)
When flexural systems or members, including beams and slabs, are load tested for moment resistance,
they shall have a deflection recovery, within 24 h of removal of the test load, as follows:
Non-prestressed members
First test
60%
Retest
75%
Prestressed members
80%

Section 20.3.2.3 - In the case of two-way slabs, the central slab deflection shall be measured relative to the deflection at (Starts on Page 162)
the supporting columns or walls.

Section 20.3.2.4 - Immediately before application of the test load, the necessary initial readings shall be made as a datum (Starts on Page 162)
for the measurements of deflections caused by the application of the test load.

Section 20.3.2.6 - Following the action specified in Clause 20.3.2.5, the test load shall be removed. Deflection readings (Starts on Page 162)
shall be taken 24 h after removal of the test load.

Section 20.3.2.7 - Retests of non-prestressed construction shall not be conducted until 72 h after removal of the first test (Starts on Page 162)
load.
21 Special provisions for seismic design

Section 21.1 - Scope (Starts on Page 162)
Clauses 21.2 to 21.11 specify requirements for the design and construction of reinforced concrete
members of structures for which the design earthquake forces have been determined on the basis of
energy dissipation from the non-linear response of the seismic-force-resisting system (SFRS).
reproduction interdite.

Section 21.2.1 - Capacity design (Starts on Page 163)
The structures identified in Clause 21.1 shall be the subject of capacity design. In the capacity design of
structures, kinematically consistent mechanisms are chosen, and the energy-dissipating elements are
designed and detailed as specified in Clauses 21.2 to 21.8. All other structural elements in the SFRS are
then provided with sufficient reserve capacity to ensure that the chosen energy-dissipating mechanisms
are maintained in the selected locations throughout the deformations that can occur.

Section 21.2.2 - Seismic-force-resisting systems (Starts on Page 163)
Clauses 21.2 to 21.8 specify requirements covering the design and detailing of the standard seismic-
force-resisting systems (SFRSs) identified in the National Building Code of Canada (NBCC).
Clauses 21.2 to 21.8 were developed for the design of individual SFRSs that are continuous over the full
building height and do not have significant discontinuity in strength or stiffness.
When an SFRS has a strength, stiffness, or geometrical irregularity as defined by the NBCC, or when
combinations of SFRSs acting in the same direction are not continuous over the building height, or when
elements from two or more SFRS types are combined to create a hybrid system, the design of the SFRS
and gravity-load resisting frame shall account for the actual inelastic behaviour of the SFRS. An
evaluation shall be carried out to
a)
verify the compatibility of the system(s);
b)
confirm the assumed inelastic (energy-dissipating) mechanism(s);
c)
confirm that the inelastic demands are less than the inelastic capacities; and
d)
account for redistribution of forces.
The requirement for such an evaluation may be waived if the performance has been previously verified
by experimental evidence and analysis.
A reinforced concrete seismic-force-resisting system other than those specified in Clauses 21.2 to 21.8
may be used as an alternative solution if it can be demonstrated through testing, research, and analysis
that the seismic performance of the structural system is at least equivalent to that of a standard SFRS
that meets the requirements of Clauses 21.2 to 21.8.
Note: The requirements for determining Rd and Ro of the system are given in the National Building Code of Canada
and Commentary J, and the Compliance requirements for alternative solutions are given in Division A of NBCC.

Section 21.2.3 - Design based on nonlinear dynamic analysis (Starts on Page 163)
Nonlinear dynamic analysis used for the basis of design shall include a sufficient number of appropriate
ground motion time histories and shall include appropriate nonlinear material models for the structural
elements that consider the reversed cyclic loading characteristics such as strength and stiffness
degradation, ductility capacity and general hysteretic response. The nonlinear analysis and resulting
design shall be reviewed by a qualified independent review panel.
Note: The National Building Code of Canada and Commentary J provide the requirements for a special study using
nonlinear dynamic analysis. The 2011 LATBSDC (Los Angeles Tall Buildings Structural Design Council) Alternate
Procedure for Seismic Analysis and Design of Tall Buildings Located in the Los Angeles Area provides the
requirements for a qualified independent review panel.
reproduction interdite.

Section 21.2.4.1 - The requirements of Clauses 1 to 18 and 23 shall apply to the design and detailing of structural (Starts on Page 164)
members unless modified by the requirements of Clause 21.

Section 21.2.4.2 - Structural members below the base of the structure that are intended to transmit earthquake-induced (Starts on Page 164)
forces to the foundation shall meet the requirements of Clause 21.

Section 21.2.4.3 - Regardless of the type of seismic-force-resisting system (SFRS), all members of the structure assumed (Starts on Page 164)
not to be part of the SFRS might be required to satisfy Clause 21.11 depending on the value of
IEFaSa(0.2) and the calculated maximum interstorey drift as given in Clause 21.11.1.1.

Section 21.2.5.1 - The interaction of all structural and non-structural elements that materially affect the linear and non- (Starts on Page 164)
linear response of the structure to earthquake motions shall be considered in the analysis.

shall be used. The effective section property to be used as a fraction of the gross section property shall
be as specified in Table 21.1.

Section 21.2.7.1.1 - Reinforcement for SFRS designed with a force modification factor, Rd, greater than 2.5 shall be weldable (Starts on Page 165)
grade in compliance with CSA G30.18. Reinforcement for SFRS designed with a force modification factor,

Section 21.2.7.1.2 - The design, detailing, and ductility requirements for structures designed using a reinforcement grade (Starts on Page 165)
greater than 400 shall account for the increased strain demands.
Note: The procedures specified in Clause 21, with the exception of Clause 21.2.8.2 were developed for Grade 400
reinforcement. The additional strains required for higher yield-strength steel will generally reduce ductility.

Section 21.2.7.2.1 - Clause 12.2.5, which permits a reduction of lap splice length when the area of reinforcing steel provided (Starts on Page 165)
exceeds the area required, shall not apply to reinforcement resisting earthquake-induced forces.
reproduction interdite.

Section 21.2.7.3.1 - Mechanical splices shall be classified as either Type 1 or Type 2, as follows: (Starts on Page 166)

b)

Section 21.2.7.3.2 - Type 1 mechanical splices shall not be used within a distance equal to twice the member depth from (Starts on Page 166)
the column or beam face or from sections where yielding of the reinforcement is likely to occur as a
result of inelastic lateral displacements. Type 2 mechanical splices may be used in any location.

Section 21.2.7.3.3 - Restrictions or special requirements for mechanical splices are given in Clause 21.5.4.2 for moderately (Starts on Page 166)
ductile and ductile walls; Clause 21.8.2.1 for ductile moment-resisting frames constructed using precast
concrete; and Clause 21.9.4.4 for structural diaphragms.

Section 21.2.7.4.1 - Welded splices in reinforcement resisting earthquake-induced forces shall comply with Clause 12.14.3.3 (Starts on Page 166)
and shall not be used within a distance equal to twice the member depth from the column or beam
face or from sections where yielding of the reinforcement is likely to occur as a result of inelastic lateral
displacements.

Section 21.2.7.4.2 - Welding of stirrups, ties, inserts, or similar elements to longitudinal reinforcement that is required by (Starts on Page 166)
design shall not be permitted.

Section 21.3.1.5.1 - Design forces (Starts on Page 169)
The factored shear resistance of frame members shall be at least equal to the shear determined by
assuming that moments equal to the probable moment resistance act at the faces of the joint so as to
produce maximum shear in the member, and that the member is then loaded with the tributary
transverse load along the span. The moments corresponding to probable resistance shall be calculated
using the properties of the member at the faces of the joint. The factored shear need not exceed that
determined from factored load combinations, with load effects calculated using RdRo equal to 1.3.

Section 21.3.1.5.2 - Shear reinforcement (Starts on Page 169)

a)
the values of Œ∏ = 45¬∞ and Œ≤ = 0 shall be used in the regions specified in Clause 21.3.1.4.1; and
b)
transverse reinforcement required to resist shear shall be hoops over the lengths of members, as
specified in Clause 21.3.1.4.1.

Section 21.4.1 - General (Starts on Page 174)
The requirements specified in Clause 21.4 shall apply to moment-resisting frames SFRS designed using a
force modification factor, Rd, of 2.5.

Section 21.4.2.1 - Beams (Starts on Page 174)
Beams shall satisfy the following dimensional limitations:
a)
the clear span of the member shall be not less than three times its effective depth;
b)
the width-to-depth ratio of the cross-section shall be not less than 0.3; and
c)
the width shall be not less than 250 mm and not more than the width of the supporting member
(measured on a plane perpendicular to the longitudinal axis of the flexural member) plus distances
on each side of the supporting member not exceeding the smaller of the width of the supporting
member or three-quarters of the depth of the supporting member.

Section 21.4.2.2 - Columns (Starts on Page 174)
Columns shall satisfy the following dimensional limitations:
a)
the shortest cross-sectional dimension, measured on a straight line passing through the geometric
centroid, shall be not less than 250 mm; and
reproduction interdite.
b)
the ratio of the shortest cross-sectional dimension to the perpendicular dimension shall be not less
than 0.4.

Section 21.4.3.1 - The positive moment resistance at the face of the joint shall be not less than one-third of the negative (Starts on Page 175)
moment resistance provided at that face of the joint. Neither the negative nor the positive moment
resistance at any section along the length of the member shall be less than one-fifth of the maximum
moment resistance provided at the face of either joint.

Section 21.4.3.2 - At both ends of the member, 10M or larger stirrups detailed as hoops shall be provided over lengths (Starts on Page 175)
equal to twice the member depth measured from the face of the supporting member toward mid-span.
The first stirrup shall be located not more than 50 mm from the face of the supporting member and the
spacing shall not exceed the smallest of
a)
d/4;
b)
eight times the diameter of the smallest longitudinal bar enclosed;
c)
24 times the diameter of the stirrup bar; or
d)
300 mm.

Section 21.4.4.2 - The sum of the factored flexural resistances of the column sections framing into a joint, accounting for (Starts on Page 175)
axial loads, shall exceed the sum of the nominal flexural resistances of the beams framing into the same
joint. In T-beam construction where the slab is in tension under moments at the face of the joint, slab
reinforcement within an effective slab width as specified in Clause 10.3 shall be assumed to contribute
to flexural resistance if the slab reinforcement is developed at the critical section for flexure.
Flexural resistances shall be summed in such a manner that the column moments oppose the beam
moments. This requirement shall be satisfied for beam moments acting in either direction. The design
column forces need not exceed those determined from the factored load combinations, with factored
load effects calculated using RdRo equal to 1.3.

Section 21.4.4.3 - Confinement reinforcement in accordance with Clause 21.2.8.2 shall be provided at both ends of the (Starts on Page 175)
columns over a length equal to the largest of one-sixth of the clear height, the maximum cross-sectional
dimension, or 450 mm, with a spacing not exceeding the smallest of
a)
eight longitudinal bar diameters;
b)
24 tie diameters; or
c)
one-half of the minimum column dimension.
reproduction interdite.

Section 21.4.4.4 - In the direction perpendicular to the longitudinal axis of the column, crossties or legs of overlapping (Starts on Page 176)
hoops shall have centre-to-centre spacings not exceeding 350 mm.

Section 21.4.4.5 - Columns that can develop plastic hinges because of their connection to rigid members such as transfer (Starts on Page 176)
girders, foundations or discontinued walls, or because of their position at the base of the structure,
shall be provided with transverse confinement reinforcement over their clear height in accordance with
the requirements of Clause 21.2.8.2.

Section 21.4.5 - Shear in frames (Starts on Page 176)
The factored shear resistance of beams and columns resisting earthquake effects shall be not less than
the lesser of
a)
the sum of the maximum shear associated with development of nominal moment resistances of
the member at each restrained end of the clear span and the shear calculated using earthquake
load combinations for gravity loads; or
b)
the maximum shear obtained from factored load combinations, with factored load effects
calculated using RdRo equal to 1.3.

Section 21.4.6.1 - The design shear forces acting in a beam column joint shall be those induced by the nominal resistance (Starts on Page 176)
of the beams or the columns framing into the joint, whichever is less, except that they need not exceed
those determined from the factored load combinations, with factored load effects calculated using RdRo
equal to 1.3. Where beams frame into the joint from two directions, each direction may be considered
independently.

Section 21.4.6.2 - The factored shear resistance of the joint shall not exceed the following, where f‚Ä≤ (Starts on Page 176)

concrete in the joint:
a)
for confined joints:

c)
for other joints:
.
A member that frames into a face shall be considered to provide confinement to the joint if at least
three-quarters of the face of the joint is covered by the framing member. A joint shall be considered to
be confined if such confining members frame into all faces of the joint.

Section 21.4.6.3 - The amount of transverse reinforcement in each joint shall not be less than (Starts on Page 176)
a)
the larger of the amount of transverse reinforcement provided in the column above the joint and
the column below the joint; or
b)
one half of the amount required in Item a) for confined joints as defined in Clause 21.4.6.2.
reproduction interdite.
